Institute","authoringStatusCode":"in_progress","primaryImage":{"createdPrincipalId":"up016bb0b82173860f","height":402,"cloudinaryVersionId":1396428863,"id":"ci019b6c020f68860d","objectType":"ContentPhoto","title":"Hank-Aaron-9173497-2-402","primaryPhotoId":"ci019b6c020f68860d","hasFaces":true,"width":402,"publicId":"MTE4MDAzNDEwNDI3NDE0MDMw","format":"jpg","createdTimestamp":"2014-04-02T08:54:24Z","primaryContentSiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e","bytes":35254,"primarySiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e"},"vocabularyId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e:schools","editedByPrincipalId":"up016bb0b82173860f","editedTimestamp":"2014-04-02T08:54:27Z","disqusId":"6563d208-ba44-11e3-9a95-002219604f34","originalPublicationTimestamp":"2014-04-02T08:51:23Z","createdTimestamp":"2014-04-02T08:54:27Z","versionId":"1","primaryImageId":"ci019b6c020f68860d","path":"/people/groups/famous-alumni-of-josephine-allen-institute","isWorkInProgress":false,"slug":"famous-alumni-of-josephine-allen-institute","displayName":"Josephine Allen Institute","primaryContentSiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e","promoTitle":"Famous Josephine Allen Institute Alumni","primarySiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e","statusCode":"complete"}],"deathYearCe":true,"gender":"Male","birthCirca":false,"birthMonth":"2","metaDescription":"Learn more about baseball legend and Hank Aaron, including how he became a home-run leader and why his record-breaking game caused nationwide tension, at Biography.com.","editedTimestamp":"2017-02-10T20:49:00Z","authoringStatusCode":"in_progress","profileTml":"<!-- tml-version=\"2\" --><section tml-anchor=\"synopsis\"><h2>Synopsis<\/h2><p>Born into humble circumstances on February 5, 1934, in Mobile, Alabama, Hank Aaron ascended the ranks of the Negro Leagues to become a Major League Baseball icon. Aaron spent most of his 23 MLB seasons as an outfielder for the Milwaukee and Atlanta Braves, during which time he set many records, including a career total of 755 home runs. He was elected to the Baseball Hall of Fame in 1982.<\/p><\/section><div tml-anchor=\"image-hank-aaron-9173497-1-raw\">\n    <div tml-image=\"ci01a87e1d66eb860f\" tml-image-caption=\"\" tml-render-layout=\"inline\"><\/div>\n<\/div><section tml-anchor=\"background-baseball-icon\"><h2>Background: Baseball Icon<\/h2><p>American baseball icon Hank Aaron, nicknamed \"Hammerin' Hank,\" is widely regarded as one of the greatest hitters in the history of the sport. He spent 21 years as an  outfielder for the Milwaukee and Atlanta Braves and two final years as a DH for the Milwaukee Brewers, setting several  records and winning a number of honors along the way.<\/p> <p>Aaron continues to  hold many of baseball's most distinguished records today, including runs  batted in (2,297), extra-base hits (1,477), total bases (6,856) and  most years with 30 or more home runs (15). He also ranks among the Top 5 in career hits (3,771) and runs (2,174). For more than three decades, Aaron held the  record for most career home runs with 755. <a href=\"/people/barry-bonds-9542459\">Barry Bonds<\/a> broke the record on  August 7, 2007, when he hit his 756th home run in San Francisco, California.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"early-life\"><h2>Early Life<\/h2><p>Born Henry Louis Aaron on February 5, 1934, in a poor black section of Mobile, Alabama, called \"Down the Bay,\" Hank  Aaron was the third of eight children born to Estella and Herbert Aaron,  who made a living as a tavern owner and a dry dock boilermaker's  assistant.<\/p> <p>Aaron and  his family moved to the middle-class Toulminville neighborhood when he  was 8 years old. Aaron developed a strong affinity for baseball and football at a young age, and tended to focus more heavily on sports than his  studies. During his freshman and  sophomore years, he attended Central High School, a segregated high school in Mobile, where he excelled  at both football and baseball. On the baseball diamond, he played  shortstop and  third base.<\/p> <p>In his junior year, Aaron transferred to the  Josephine Allen Institute, a neighboring private school that had an  organized baseball program. Before the end of his first year at Allen,  he had more than proved his abilities on the baseball field. Then, perhaps sensing that he had a bigger  future ahead of him, in  1951, the 18-year-old Aaron quit school to play for the Negro Baseball League's Indianapolis Clowns.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"major-leagues\"><h2>Major Leagues<\/h2><p>It wasn't a long stay. After leading his club to victory in the league's 1952 World Series, Aaron was recruited by the Milwaukee Braves (formerly of Boston and  later of Atlanta) for $10,000. The Braves assigned their new player to one of their  farm clubs, the Eau Claire Bears. Again, Aaron did not disappoint, earning Northern League Rookie of the Year honors.<\/p> <p>Hank Aaron made his Major League debut in 1954, at age 20, when  a spring training injury to a Braves outfielder created a roster spot  for him. Following a solid first year (he hit .280 with 13 home runs), Aaron charged  through the 1955 season with a blend of power (27 home runs), run  production (106 runs batted in) and average (.328) that would come to  define his long career. In 1956, after winning the first of two  batting titles, Aaron registered an outstanding 1957 season, taking home  the National League MVP and nearly nabbing the Triple Crown by hitting  44 home runs, knocking in another 132 and batting .322.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"star-player\"><h2>Star Player<\/h2><p>That same year, Aaron demonstrated his ability to come up big  when it counted most. His 11th inning home run in late September  propelled the Braves to the World Series, where he led underdog  Milwaukee to an upset win over the New York Yankees in seven games. <\/p><p>With the game still years away from the multimillion-dollar contracts handed to star players, Aaron's annual pay in 1959  was around $30,000. When he equaled that amount that same year in  endorsements, Aaron realized there could be more in store for him if he  continued to hit for power. \"I noticed that they never had a show called  'Singles Derby,'\" he once explained.<\/p><p>He was right, of course, and over the next decade and a half,  the always-fit Aaron banged out 30 to 40 home runs on an annual basis. In 1973, at the age of 39, Aaron was still a force, clubbing 40 home runs to finish just one behind <a href=\"/people/babe-ruth-9468009\">Babe Ruth<\/a>'s hallowed career mark of 714.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"obstacles\"><h2>Obstacles<\/h2><p>The chase to beat the Babe's record revealed that world of baseball was far from being free of the racial tensions that prevailed around it. Letters poured into the Braves offices, as many as 3,000 a day for Aaron. Some wrote to congratulate him, but many others were appalled that a black man should break baseball's most sacred record. Death threats were a part of the mix.<\/p><p>Still, Aaron pushed forward. He didn't try to inflame the atmosphere, but he didn't keep his mouth shut, either, speaking out against the league's lack of ownership and management opportunities for minorities. \"On the field, blacks have been able to be super giants,\" he once stated. \"But, once our playing days are over, this is the end of it and we go back to the back of the bus again.\"<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"legacy\"><h2>Legacy<\/h2><p>In 1974, after tying the Babe on Opening Day in Cincinnati, Ohio, Aaron came home with his team. On April 8, he banged out his record 715th home run at 9:07 p.m. in the fourth inning against the Los Angeles Dodgers. It was a triumph and a relief, as more than 50,000 fans on hand cheered him on as he rounded the bases. There were fireworks and a band, and when he crossed home plate, Aaron's parents were there to greet him. <\/p><p>Overall, Aaron finished the 1974 season with 20 home runs. He played two more years, moving back to Milwaukee to finish out his career with the Brewers in the same city that propelled him to stardom. <\/p><p>After retiring as a player, Aaron moved into the Atlanta Braves front office as executive vice president, where he became a leading spokesman for minority hiring in baseball. He was elected to the Baseball Hall of Fame in 1982. His autobiography, <em>I Had a Hammer<\/em>, was published in 1990.<\/p><p>In 1999, to celebrate the 25th anniversary of Aaron surpassing Ruth's record, Major League Baseball announced the formation of the Hank Aaron Award, given annually to the best overall hitter in each league. In 2002, he was honored with the Presidential Medal of Freedom.<\/p><p>Slowed by hip replacement surgery in 2014, Aaron nevertheless made it to a ceremony in January 2016 in which he was awarded the Japanese Order of the Rising Sun, Gold Rays with Rosette. He was honored for his close relationship to Japanese baseball legend Sadaharu Oh, and for his efforts to promote the two countries' shared love of the game. <\/p><\/section><section 0">&ldquo;On the field, blacks have been able to be super giants. But, once our playing days are over, this is the end of it and  we go back to the back of the bus again.&rdquo;</div></div><div class="text-carousel--author">&#151;Hank Aaron</div></div></phx-text-carousel></aside><div class="m-aside-rail l-one-third m-person--ad" phx-breakout-flow-force="true" phx-detail-sidebar=".m-detail--body" ng-cloak><div class="m-aside-rail--content" phx-reflow-on-height-change><aside phx-breakout-flow><div class="m-sidebar-ad not-size-A not-size-B" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;300x600&quot;,&quot;300x900&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;m-sponsor--adjacency&quot;]}]"><div class="m-sidebar-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-B"><div id="ad-19025a1dcda949d9a0a7a9afa105bb56"></div></div></div></aside><aside class="m-aside-list"><phx-reading-list class="m-reading-list"></phx-reading-list></aside></div></div><section class="m-detail--body" phx-tml-hybrid-renderer><!-- tml-version="2" --><section id="synopsis"><h2>Synopsis</h2><p>Born into humble circumstances on February 5, 1934, in Mobile, Alabama, Hank Aaron ascended the ranks of the Negro Leagues to become a Major League Baseball icon. Aaron spent most of his 23 MLB seasons as an outfielder for the Milwaukee and Atlanta Braves,&#xA0;during which time he set many records, including a career total of 755 home runs. He was elected to the Baseball Hall of Fame in 1982.</p></section><div id="image-hank-aaron-9173497-1-raw">

</div><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" phx-inline-ad-layout="f3f17b6c953f495d815a17fa9948da08" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-f3f17b6c953f495d815a17fa9948da08"></div></div></div></aside><section id="background-baseball-icon"><h2>Background: Baseball Icon</h2><p>American baseball icon Hank Aaron, nicknamed &quot;Hammerin&apos; Hank,&quot; is widely regarded as one of the greatest hitters in the history of the sport. He spent 21 years as an  outfielder for the Milwaukee and Atlanta Braves and two final years as a DH for the Milwaukee Brewers, setting several  records and winning a number of honors along the way.</p> <p>Aaron continues to  hold many of baseball&apos;s most distinguished records today, including runs  batted in (2,297), extra-base hits (1,477), total bases (6,856) and  most years with 30 or more home runs (15). He also ranks among the Top 5 in career hits (3,771) and runs (2,174). For more than three decades, Aaron held the  record for most career home runs with 755. <a href="/people/barry-bonds-9542459">Barry Bonds</a> broke the record on  August 7, 2007, when he hit his 756th home run in San Francisco, California.</p></section><section id="early-life"><h2>Early Life</h2><p>Born Henry Louis Aaron on February 5, 1934, in a poor black section of Mobile, Alabama, called &quot;Down the Bay,&quot; Hank  Aaron was the third of eight children born to Estella and Herbert Aaron,  who made a living as a tavern owner and a dry dock boilermaker&apos;s  assistant.</p><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-A not-size-B" phx-inline-ad-layout="f1e3ab55c62f4b0dbac2825ddbc39797" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-B" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;970x250&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-970x250&quot;]},{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;,&quot;l-breakout&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-B"><div id="ad-f1e3ab55c62f4b0dbac2825ddbc39797"></div></div></div><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-B m-in-content-ad-row--bonus" phx-bonus-ad="{&quot;companionSlotId&quot;:&quot;f1e3ab55c62f4b0dbac2825ddbc39797&quot;,&quot;requiredSizeCode&quot;:&quot;300x250&quot;,&quot;slotConfig&quot;:{&quot;id&quot;:&quot;11e41fead5754711bb6813e2eacc4662&quot;,&quot;config&quot;:{&quot;sizes&quot;:[[300,250]]},&quot;screenSizes&quot;:[&quot;C&quot;,&quot;D&quot;],&quot;model&quot;:{&quot;zone&quot;:&quot;in_content&quot;,&quot;index&quot;:&quot;1&quot;}}}"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-B"><div id="ad-11e41fead5754711bb6813e2eacc4662"></div></div></div></aside><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D" phx-inline-ad-layout="cf140ab462374903a464d5465cd613c2" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;,&quot;l-breakout&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-cf140ab462374903a464d5465cd613c2"></div></div></div><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D m-in-content-ad-row--bonus" phx-bonus-ad="{&quot;companionSlotId&quot;:&quot;cf140ab462374903a464d5465cd613c2&quot;,&quot;requiredSizeCode&quot;:&quot;300x250&quot;,&quot;slotConfig&quot;:{&quot;id&quot;:&quot;345aa50e7d3c40aa901186a97f158a26&quot;,&quot;config&quot;:{&quot;sizes&quot;:[[300,250]]},&quot;screenSizes&quot;:[&quot;B&quot;],&quot;model&quot;:{&quot;zone&quot;:&quot;in_content&quot;,&quot;index&quot;:&quot;1&quot;}}}"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-345aa50e7d3c40aa901186a97f158a26"></div></div></div></aside> <p>Aaron and  his family moved to the middle-class Toulminville neighborhood when he  was 8 years old. Aaron developed a strong affinity for baseball and football at a young age, and tended to focus more heavily on sports than his  studies. During his freshman and  sophomore years, he attended Central High School, a segregated high school in Mobile, where he excelled  at both football and baseball. On the baseball diamond, he played  shortstop and  third base.</p><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" phx-inline-ad-layout="4ad78a4412c44dd3a458758052247fdb" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-4ad78a4412c44dd3a458758052247fdb"></div></div></div></aside> <p>In his junior year, Aaron transferred to the  Josephine Allen Institute, a neighboring private school that had an  organized baseball program. Before the end of his first year at Allen,  he had more than proved his abilities on the baseball field. Then, perhaps sensing that he had a bigger  future ahead of him, in  1951, the 18-year-old Aaron quit school to play for the Negro Baseball League&apos;s Indianapolis Clowns.</p></section><section id="major-leagues"><h2>Major Leagues</h2><p>It wasn&apos;t a long stay. After leading his club to victory in the league&apos;s 1952 World Series, Aaron was recruited by the Milwaukee Braves (formerly of Boston and  later of Atlanta) for $10,000. The Braves assigned their new player to one of their  farm clubs, the Eau Claire Bears. Again, Aaron did not disappoint, earning Northern League Rookie of the Year honors.</p> <p>Hank Aaron made his Major League debut in 1954, at age 20, when  a spring training injury to a Braves outfielder created a roster spot  for him. Following a solid first year (he hit .280 with 13 home runs), Aaron charged  through the 1955 season with a blend of power (27 home runs), run  production (106 runs batted in) and average (.328) that would come to  define his long career. In 1956, after winning the first of two  batting titles, Aaron registered an outstanding 1957 season, taking home  the National League MVP and nearly nabbing the Triple Crown by hitting  44 home runs, knocking in another 132 and batting .322.</p><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" phx-inline-ad-layout="1ed97579c97e4f58876538cf0f8199e0" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-1ed97579c97e4f58876538cf0f8199e0"></div></div></div></aside></section><section id="star-player"><h2>Star Player</h2><p>That same year, Aaron demonstrated his ability to come up big  when it counted most. His 11th inning home run in late September  propelled the Braves to the World Series, where he led underdog  Milwaukee to an upset win over the New York Yankees in seven games. </p><p>With the game still years away from the multimillion-dollar contracts handed to star players, Aaron&apos;s annual pay in 1959  was around $30,000. When he equaled that amount that same year in  endorsements, Aaron realized there could be more in store for him if he  continued to hit for power. &quot;I noticed that they never had a show called  &apos;Singles Derby,&apos;&quot; he once explained.</p><p>He was right, of course, and over the next decade and a half,  the always-fit Aaron banged out 30 to 40 home runs on an annual basis. In 1973, at the age of 39, Aaron was still a force, clubbing 40 home runs to finish just one behind <a href="/people/babe-ruth-9468009">Babe Ruth</a>&apos;s hallowed career mark of 714.</p></section><section id="obstacles"><h2>Obstacles</h2><p>The chase to beat the Babe&apos;s record revealed that world of baseball was far from being free of the racial tensions that prevailed around it. Letters poured into the Braves offices, as many as 3,000 a day for Aaron. Some wrote to congratulate him, but many others were appalled that a black man should break baseball&apos;s most sacred record. Death threats were a part of the mix.</p><p>Still, Aaron pushed forward. He didn&apos;t try to inflame the atmosphere, but he didn&apos;t keep his mouth shut, either, speaking out against the league&apos;s lack of ownership and management opportunities for minorities. &quot;On the field, blacks have been able to be super giants,&quot; he once stated. &quot;But, once our playing days are over, this is the end of it and we go back to the back of the bus again.&quot;</p><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" phx-inline-ad-layout="d2eb876e859e4fbaab265f7f733a4f9e" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-d2eb876e859e4fbaab265f7f733a4f9e"></div></div></div></aside></section><section id="legacy"><h2>Legacy</h2><p>In 1974, after tying the Babe on Opening Day in Cincinnati, Ohio, Aaron came home with his team. On April 8, he banged out his record 715th home run at 9:07 p.m. in the fourth inning against the Los Angeles Dodgers. It was a triumph and a relief, as more than 50,000 fans on hand cheered him on as he rounded the bases. There were fireworks and a band, and when he crossed home plate, Aaron&apos;s parents were there to greet him. </p><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-A not-size-B" phx-inline-ad-layout="b2433d255962420cb6b3674123929667" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-B" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;970x250&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-970x250&quot;]},{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;,&quot;l-breakout&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-B"><div id="ad-b2433d255962420cb6b3674123929667"></div></div></div><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-B m-in-content-ad-row--bonus" phx-bonus-ad="{&quot;companionSlotId&quot;:&quot;b2433d255962420cb6b3674123929667&quot;,&quot;requiredSizeCode&quot;:&quot;300x250&quot;,&quot;slotConfig&quot;:{&quot;id&quot;:&quot;ed6695dc21784280b2ff4ed4b3384aa2&quot;,&quot;config&quot;:{&quot;sizes&quot;:[[300,250]]},&quot;screenSizes&quot;:[&quot;C&quot;,&quot;D&quot;],&quot;model&quot;:{&quot;zone&quot;:&quot;in_content&quot;,&quot;index&quot;:&quot;3&quot;}}}"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-B"><div id="ad-ed6695dc21784280b2ff4ed4b3384aa2"></div></div></div></aside><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D" phx-inline-ad-layout="572f61486d154276b614d0f50cd1b531" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;,&quot;l-breakout&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-572f61486d154276b614d0f50cd1b531"></div></div></div><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D m-in-content-ad-row--bonus" phx-bonus-ad="{&quot;companionSlotId&quot;:&quot;572f61486d154276b614d0f50cd1b531&quot;,&quot;requiredSizeCode&quot;:&quot;300x250&quot;,&quot;slotConfig&quot;:{&quot;id&quot;:&quot;f51de932896747b9947c3177e265766b&quot;,&quot;config&quot;:{&quot;sizes&quot;:[[300,250]]},&quot;screenSizes&quot;:[&quot;B&quot;],&quot;model&quot;:{&quot;zone&quot;:&quot;in_content&quot;,&quot;index&quot;:&quot;3&quot;}}}"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-A not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-f51de932896747b9947c3177e265766b"></div></div></div></aside><p>Overall, Aaron finished the 1974 season with 20 home runs. He played two more years, moving back to Milwaukee to finish out his career with the Brewers in the same city that propelled him to stardom. </p><p>After retiring as a player, Aaron moved into the Atlanta Braves front office as executive vice president, where he became a leading spokesman for minority hiring in baseball. He was elected to the Baseball Hall of Fame in 1982. His autobiography, <em>I Had a Hammer</em>, was published in 1990.</p><p>In 1999, to celebrate the 25th anniversary of Aaron surpassing Ruth&apos;s record, Major League Baseball announced the formation of the Hank Aaron Award, given annually to the best overall hitter in each league. In 2002, he was honored with the&#xA0;Presidential Medal of Freedom.</p><p>Slowed by hip replacement surgery in 2014, Aaron nevertheless made it to a ceremony in January 2016 in which he was awarded the Japanese Order of the Rising Sun, Gold Rays with Rosette. He was honored for his close relationship to Japanese baseball legend Sadaharu Oh, and for his efforts to promote the two countries&apos; shared love of the game.&#xA0;</p><aside class="m-in-content-ad-row l-inline not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" phx-inline-ad-layout="caf328c6cecb49e2a720e6cf43d5cae2" phx-breakout-dam=""><div class="m-in-content-ad not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D" data-class-rules="[{&quot;sizes&quot;:[&quot;adaptive&quot;],&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;is-adaptive&quot;]}]"><div class="m-in-content-ad--slot is-placeholder not-size-B not-size-C not-size-D"><div id="ad-caf328c6cecb49e2a720e6cf43d5cae2"></div></div></div></aside></section><section id="related-video-gallery"><h2>Related Videos</h2>

States","primarySiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e","statusCode":"complete"},"birthCirca":true,"birthMonth":"5","metaDescription":"Learn more about Chicago serial killer H.H. Holmes and his notorious 'Murder Castle,' at Biography.com.","editedTimestamp":"2017-04-28T04:47:57Z","deathState":{"publicationTimestamp":"2014-04-02T04:32:58Z","createdPrincipalId":"up016bb0b82173860f","commentsEnabled":true,"id":"ci019b6c01fefe860d","objectType":"ContentRichTerm","isNoindex":false,"title":"Pennsylvania","authoringStatusCode":"in_progress","primaryImage":{"createdPrincipalId":"up016bb0b82173860f","height":402,"cloudinaryVersionId":1396413349,"id":"ci01a33b79f0dd860e","objectType":"ContentPhoto","title":"Chaim-Potok-37137-1-402","primaryPhotoId":"ci01a33b79f0dd860e","hasFaces":true,"width":402,"publicId":"MTE5NDg0MDU0NTg4MTk2MzY3","format":"jpg","createdTimestamp":"2014-04-02T04:35:51Z","primaryContentSiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e","bytes":79191,"primarySiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e"},"vocabularyId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e:death_state","editedByPrincipalId":"up016bb0b82173860f","editedTimestamp":"2014-04-02T04:35:55Z","disqusId":"479e5bae-ba20-11e3-8d07-002219604f34","originalPublicationTimestamp":"2014-04-02T04:32:58Z","createdTimestamp":"2014-04-02T04:35:55Z","versionId":"1","primaryImageId":"ci01a33b79f0dd860e","path":"/people/groups/died-in-pennsylvania","isWorkInProgress":false,"slug":"died-in-pennsylvania","displayName":"Pennsylvania","primaryContentSiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e","promoTitle":"Famous People Who Died in Pennsylvania","primarySiteId":"cs01a33b78d5c5860e","statusCode":"complete"},"authoringStatusCode":"in_progress","profileTml":"<!-- tml-version=\"2\" --><section tml-anchor=\"synopsis\"><h2>Synopsis<\/h2><p>Born in 1861 in Gilmanton, New Hampshire, H.H. Holmes was one of America's first serial murderers. He took over a Chicago pharmacy and built it into an elaborate maze of death traps to which he lured numerous victims during the 1893 Columbian Exposition. He was eventually captured and hanged in 1896. Erik Larson wrote about Holmes in the book <em>The Devil in the White City<\/em>, published in 2003.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"early-life\"><h2>Early Life<\/h2><p>Infamous con artist and serial killer H.H. Holmes was born Herman Webster Mudgett on May 16, 1861, in Gilmanton, New Hampshire. Sometimes referred as the \"Beast of Chicago,\" H.H. Holmes killed many of the city's inhabitants in his specially constructed home, which was later nicknamed the \"Murder Castle.\" He has also been linked to deaths in other parts of the United States and Canada.<\/p><p>Born to an affluent family, Holmes had a privileged childhood. It has been said that he appeared to be unusually intelligent at an early age. Still there were haunting signs of what was to come. He expressed an interest in medicine, which reportedly led him to practice surgery on animals. Some accounts indicate that he may have been responsible for the death of a friend.<\/p><p>Holmes's life of crime began with various frauds and scams. As a medical student at the University of Michigan, he stole corpses, which he used to make false insurance claims. Holmes may have used the bodies for experiments, as well.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"the-murder-castle-is-built\"><h2>The 'Murder Castle' is Built<\/h2><p>In 1886, H.H. Holmes moved to Chicago, Illinois. He soon found work in a pharmacy, using his now infamous alias, Dr. Henry H. Holmes. Eventually he took over the business and its original owner mysteriously disappeared. Holmes had a three-story building constructed, creating an elaborate house of horrors. The upper floors contained his living quarters and many small rooms where he tortured and killed his victims. Some of these rooms had gas jets so that Holmes could asphyxiate his victims. There were also trapdoors and chutes so that he could move the bodies down to the basement where he could burn his victims’ remains in a kiln there or dispose of them in other ways.<\/p><p>During the 1893 Columbian Exposition, Holmes opened up his home as a hotel for visitors to the world's fair. Unfortunately, some of his guests did not survive his hospitality. Many of these victims—no one knows for certain the total number—were women who he seduced, swindled and then killed. Holmes had a habit of getting engaged to a woman and then for his fiancée to suddenly \"disappear.\" Others were lured there by the offer of employment.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"insurance-scams\"><h2>Insurance Scams<\/h2><p>All the while, Holmes continued to work insurance scams and it was one of these scams that led to his undoing. He joined forces with Benjamin Pitezel to collect $10,000 from a life insurance company. The two traveled around for a time committing other frauds. Landing in jail in Texas, Holmes brought fellow inmate Marion Hedgepeth—who knew Holmes as H.M. Howard—in on the life insurance scheme with Pitezel. When Holmes failed to deliver Hedgepeth's share of the deal, Hedgepeth tipped off the authorities.<\/p><p>While they eventually identified Howard as Holmes, the authorities did not catch on to Holmes soon enough to stop his final murders. He killed Pitezel and then convinced Pitezel’s widow that her husband was still alive. Becoming concerned that the five Pitezel children might expose him, he went away with three of the children, eventually killing them.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"trial-and-execution\"><h2>Trial and Execution<\/h2><p>At first, Holmes was charged with insurance fraud. He later stood trial for the murder of Benjamin Pitezel. During his time in custody, Holmes gave numerous stories to police, once admitting to killing 27 people. Estimates range from 20 to 100 victims, with some going as high as 200 victims. If Holmes even did half of the crimes associated with him, he clearly surpassed later American serial killers such as Ted Bundy and John Wayne Gacy in his depravity.<\/p><p>After his conviction, Holmes appealed his case, but lost. He met his end on May 7, 1896, when he was hanged for the Pitezel murder in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania. His life as one of America's first serial killers has been the subject of many books and documentaries, including <em>The Devil in the White City<\/em> (2003), written by Erik Larson.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"video-gallery\"><h2>Videos<\/h2><\/section><div tml-collection=\"\" tml-collection-grid=\"true\" tml-render-layout=\"inline\" Little is known of his youth, but early on he entered a life of thievery, eventually heading west and joining a violent gang. Billy was captured and sentenced to death for the murder of a sheriff, but escaped after killing guards. The legend of Billy the Kid was created by his killer, Sheriff Garrett.<\/p><\/section><section tml-anchor=\"early-life\"><h2>Early Life<\/h2><p>Billy the Kid was born William Henry McCarty Jr. on November 23, 1859, in New York City. Little is known about the early life of William McCarty (also known as Henry Antrim and William H. Bonney, an alias), but it is believed that his father died or left the family when Billy was very young, and he was orphaned at 15 when his mother died of tuberculosis. Shortly after, he and his brother got involved in petty theft.<\/p> <p>McCarty had a slim physique, sandy blond hair and blue eyes and wore a signature sugar-loaf sombrero hat with a wide decorative band. He could be charming and polite one moment, then outraged and violent the next, a quixotic nature he used to great effect during his heists and robberies. According to legend, he killed 21 men during his days as an outlaw, one for each year of his life, though he likely killed far fewer than that number.<\/p><\/section> <section tml-anchor=\"outlaw\"><h2>Outlaw<\/h2><p>On the run from the authorities, McCarty moved to Arizona briefly  before joining up with a gang of gunfighters called The Boys to fight in  the Lincoln County War. Known as \"The Kid,\" McCarty switched to the  opposition to fight with John Tunstall under the name \"the Regulators.\"<\/p> <p>Barely escaping with his life, McCarty became an outlaw and a  fugitive. He stole horse and cattle until his arrest in 1880 for the  killing of Sheriff Brady during the Lincoln County War. After being  sentenced to death, he killed his two guards and escaped in 1881. He was  hunted down and shot dead by Sheriff Patrick Garrett on July 14, 1881  in Fort Sumner, New Mexico.<\/p> <p>Shortly after the shooting, Sheriff Garrett wrote a biography of McCarty, the hugely sensationalized <em>The Authentic Life of Billy, the Kid.<\/em> The book was the first of many accounts that would turn the young outlaw into a legend of the American frontier.<\/p><\/section><section
